![](https://yt3.ggpht.com/ytc/AIdro_lxmfS93TY9eF1GqI_rzT8ROIPCil_jWCJK5NA9EW0=s88-c-k-c0x00ffffff-no-rj)

Entanglement: Pseudo-Random Wave Function initialized with same (vs. different) Seed
Most software people know what is a pseudo-random function and how its output looks random but depends on the seed with which it was initialized.
Particles interact throughout their lifetime and their quantum state is described at any time by their own unique copy of a pseudo-random wave function that was initialized by a new seed from last interaction.
After interaction, the re-initialized seed may be the same or different. If same, their pseudo-random wave-function (entirely independent instances of the same function) would generate the same random output at any given time.
Mostly the seed is different, so mostly particle-pairs are not entangled after most interactions.

[@agustinmoralesrodriguez1242](https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCECwKlaH5CrbjZLgE7vEjfw) \- But how do we verify that? The Voyager probes are the farthest manmade objects from Earth, and still nowhere near a single light year, much less more. The reason so much of physics is theoretical is because you can make the math do anything you want, but confirmation requires experimental verification. Which in cases like this we cannot achieve. Kind of like how gravity was the first fundamental force to be quantified, but even now we still debate what, exactly, it really is and how it works. Which is why we lack a means of artificially generating it.

The only problem here is the terminology “when they become entangled”. Particles are entangled they cannot become unentangled. The simplest analogy would be that of a spider web. Touch the web on one side and it’s felt on the other side. The distance between the particles is irrelevant.

![](https://yt3.ggpht.com/ytc/AIdro_mGS6OFCpJ_XV_69-HzKPkhSwp3L69Ob8Ql5JcbGnP-POA=s88-c-k-c0x00ffffff-no-rj)

There is a whole industry of people talking about "entanglement" who actually don't understand it. Take this video:
The first mistake is at [1:20](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HhUxR8fkFi4&t=80s). He says the effect is "instant". But if you know Relativity, you know there isn't a global "now" extending over the universe. There is no "instant" that's the same everywhere. Therefore nothing can happen "instantly".
The second mistake is around [1:50](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HhUxR8fkFi4&t=110s). He says that quantum objects "collapse" into one state. No they don't. The theory doesn't predict "collapse".

There's no link between them. This is no different than a pair of identical tops spun at the same rate and in the same or opposite directions. Knowing this correlation, you can tell the state of one by the state of the other so long as the correlation has not been disrupted, but doing anything to one doesn't do anything to the other. This is a common misconception.

Title: What Is Entanglement and Why Is It Important?
![Artist concept of two entangled objects. Two glowing orbs are attached by a string of light.](https://caltechsites-prod-assets.resources.caltech.edu/scienceexchange/images/Entanglement-Main-Header-Imag.2e16d0ba.fill-933x525-c100.png)

[Caltech Science Exchange](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/)

/




[Topics](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics)

/




[Quantum Science and Technology](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics/quantum-science-explained)

/



Entanglement






# What Is Entanglement and Why Is It Important?

Entanglement is at the heart of quantum physics and future quantum technologies. Like other aspects of quantum science, the phenomenon of entanglement reveals itself at very tiny, subatomic scales. When two particles, such as a pair of photons or electrons, become entangled, they remain connected even when separated by vast distances. In the same way that a ballet or tango emerges from individual dancers, entanglement arises from the connection between particles. It is what scientists call an emergent property.

* * *

### How do scientists explain quantum entanglement? [Right-click here and choose "Copy Link" to get a link to this part of the page.](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics/quantum-science-explained/entanglement\#how-do-scientists-explain-quantum-entanglement "Right-click here and choose \"Copy Link\" to get a link to this part of the page.")

In the video below, Caltech faculty members take a stab at explaining entanglement. Featured: Rana Adhikari, professor of physics; Xie Chen, professor of theoretical physics; Manuel Endres, professor of physics and Rosenberg Scholar; and John Preskill, Richard P. Feynman Professor of Theoretical Physics, Allen V. C. Davis and Lenabelle Davis Leadership Chair, and director of the Institute for Quantum Information and Matter.

How Do Scientists Explain Quantum Entanglement? - YouTube

Tap to unmute

[How Do Scientists Explain Quantum Entanglement?](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TS_gx7pUyLg) [caltech](https://www.youtube.com/channel/UClGTZDyz3CSl92TgDqIr0nw)

caltech217K subscribers

* * *

### Unbreakable Correlation [Right-click here and choose "Copy Link" to get a link to this part of the page.](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics/quantum-science-explained/entanglement\#unbreakable-correlation "Right-click here and choose \"Copy Link\" to get a link to this part of the page.")

When researchers [study entanglement](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics/quantum-science-explained/quantum-physics-experiments), they often use a special kind of crystal to generate two entangled particles from one. The entangled particles are then sent off to different locations. For this example, let's say the researchers want to measure the direction the particles are spinning, which can be either up or down along a given axis. Before the particles are measured, each will be in a state of [superposition](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics/quantum-science-explained/quantum-superposition), or both "spin up" and "spin down" at the same time.

If the researcher measures the direction of one particle's spin and then repeats the measurement on its distant, entangled partner, that researcher will always find that the pair are correlated: if one particle's spin is up, the other's will be down (the spins may instead both be up or both be down, depending on how the experiment is designed, but there will always be a correlation). Returning to our dancer metaphor, this would be like observing one dancer and finding them in a pirouette, and then automatically knowing the other dancer must also be performing a pirouette. The beauty of entanglement is that just knowing the state of one particle automatically tells you something about its companion, even when they are far apart.

### Are particles really connected across space? [Right-click here and choose "Copy Link" to get a link to this part of the page.](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics/quantum-science-explained/entanglement\#are-particles-really-connected-across-space "Right-click here and choose \"Copy Link\" to get a link to this part of the page.")

But are the particles really somehow tethered to each other across space, or is something else going on? Some scientists, including Albert Einstein in the 1930s, pointed out that the entangled particles might have always been spin up or spin down, but that this information was hidden from us until the measurements were made. Such "local hidden variable theories" argued against the mind-boggling aspect of entanglement, instead proposing that something more mundane, yet unseen, is going on.

Thanks to theoretical work by John Stewart Bell in the 1960s, and experimental work done by Caltech alumnus John Clauser (BS '64) and others beginning in the 1970s, scientists have ruled out these local hidden-variable theories. A key to the researchers' success involved observing entangled particles from different angles. In the experiment mentioned above, this means that a researcher would measure their first particle as spin up, but then use a different viewing angle (or a different spin axis direction) to measure the second particle. Rather than the two particles matching up as before, the second particle would have gone back into a state of superposition and, once observed, could be either spin up or down. The choice of the viewing angle changed the outcome of the experiment, which means that there cannot be any hidden information buried inside a particle that determines its spin before it is observed. The dance of entanglement materializes not from any one particle but from the connections between them.

### Relativity Remains Intact [Right-click here and choose "Copy Link" to get a link to this part of the page.](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics/quantum-science-explained/entanglement\#relativity-remains-intact "Right-click here and choose \"Copy Link\" to get a link to this part of the page.")

A common misconception about entanglement is that the particles are communicating with each other faster than the speed of light, which would go against Einstein's special theory of relativity. Experiments have shown that this is not true, nor can quantum physics be used to _send_ faster-than-light communications. Though scientists still debate how the seemingly bizarre phenomenon of entanglement arises, they know it is a real principle that passes test after test. In fact, while Einstein famously described entanglement as "spooky action at a distance," today's quantum scientists say there is nothing spooky about it.

"It may be tempting to think that the particles are somehow communicating with each other across these great distances, but that is not the case," says [Thomas Vidick](http://www.cms.caltech.edu/people/vidick), a professor of computing and mathematical sciences at Caltech. "There can be correlation without communication," and the particles "can be thought of as one object."

![Let&#x27;s say you have two entangled balls, each in its own box. Each ball is in a state of superposition, or both yellow and red at the same time...](https://caltechsites-prod-assets.resources.caltech.edu/scienceexchange/images/01-EntanglementPanel-C_o0fiziI.max-1000x1000.jpg)Zoom In to Image

![...until you observe the balls. If the first one is yellow, the other will be yellow. If the first one is red, the other will be red. The objects remain connected even over vast distances. Scientists think of entangled objects as really being a single object.](https://caltechsites-prod-assets.resources.caltech.edu/scienceexchange/images/02-EntanglementPanel-C.max-1000x1000.jpg)Zoom In to Image

![But what if one observer decides to look at their ball from a different angle or side of the box? The balls would revert back to a state of superposition and have a 50% chance of being yellow and 50% chance of being red.](https://caltechsites-prod-assets.resources.caltech.edu/scienceexchange/images/03-EntanglementPanel-C.max-1000x1000.jpg)Zoom In to Image

![The viewer might find a yellow ball now, even though the pair of balls had previously both been red!](https://caltechsites-prod-assets.resources.caltech.edu/scienceexchange/images/04-EntanglementPanel-C.max-1000x1000.jpg)Zoom In to Image

![Now, if the second observer also looks at their ball from the side view, it will match what the first observer saw. The ball sare still entangled, but what the viewer sees depends on how they look at the ball. This is because the entangled information about color does not lie within any one ball but exists in the connection between the balls.](https://caltechsites-prod-assets.resources.caltech.edu/scienceexchange/images/05-EntanglementPanel-C.max-1000x1000.jpg)Zoom In to Image

Credit: Lance Hayashida for Caltech Science Exchange

### Networks of Entanglement [Right-click here and choose "Copy Link" to get a link to this part of the page.](https://scienceexchange.caltech.edu/topics/quantum-science-explained/entanglement\#networks-of-entanglement "Right-click here and choose \"Copy Link\" to get a link to this part of the page.")

Entanglement can also occur among hundreds, millions, and even more particles. The phenomenon is thought to take place throughout nature, among the atoms and molecules in living species and within metals and other materials. When hundreds of particles become entangled, they still act as one unified object. Like a flock of birds, the particles become a whole entity unto itself without being in direct contact with one another. Caltech [scientists focus](https://iqim.caltech.edu/research/) on the study of these so-called many-body entangled systems, both to understand the fundamental physics and to create and develop new quantum technologies. As John Preskill, Caltech's Richard P. Feynman Professor of Theoretical Physics, Allen V. C. Davis and Lenabelle Davis Leadership Chair, and director of the Institute for Quantum Information and Matter, says, "We are making investments in and betting on entanglement being one of the most important themes of 21st-century science."

Title: ELI5: What is Quantum Entanglement? : r/explainlikeimfive - Reddit
Quantum entanglement is when particles interact in such a way that their quantum states become linked so that you can't describe the particles individually.

# What Is the Spooky Science of Quantum Entanglement?

### Learn how particles engage in a mind-boggling phenomenon that forms the backbone of quantum mechanics.

![Quantum entanglement. Conceptual artwork of a pair of entangled quantum particles or events (left and right) interacting at a distance. Quantum entanglement is one of the consequences of quantum theory. Two particles will appear to be linked across space and time, with changes to one of the particles (such as an observation or measurement) affecting the other one. This instantaneous effect appears to be independent of both space and time, meaning that, in the quantum realm, effect may precede cause.](https://assets.science.nasa.gov/dynamicimage/assets/science/bps/quantum-leaps/quantum-entanglement/Quantum_entanglement_sm.jpg?w=1200&h=831&fit=clip&crop=faces%2Cfocalpoint)

The fascinating science of quantum entanglement proposes that particles of the same origin are always connected.

GettyImages

There’s a lot we don’t know about our universe — in fact, 95% of it remains a mystery to us. That’s why scientists continue to probe our understanding of quantum physics.

There are many facets to [quantum science](https://science.nasa.gov/biological-physical/international-year-of-quantum-why-nasa-studies-the-world-we-dont-see/), but let’s zoom in on something that Albert Einstein called “spooky action at a distance”: quantum entanglement.

**What is Quantum Entanglement?**

Quantum science explores and helps explain some of the strangest phenomena in the universe, even shedding light on the mystery of dark matter and dark energy. Quantum is the study of atoms and subatomic particles, and how they interact with each other. It examines the very stuff we, and everything around us, are made of.

One of the most far-out phenomena of quantum theory is quantum entanglement, the idea that particles of the same origin, which were once connected, always stay connected. Even if they separate and move far apart in time and space, they continue to share something beyond a mere bond — they shed their original quantum states and take on a new, united quantum state which they maintain forever. This means if something happens to one particle, it affects all the others with which it’s entangled.

**A “Spooky” Science**

In 1935, Albert Einstein and colleagues first pointed out the “spooky” action of quantum entanglement. Quantum entanglement, however, appeared to conflict with Einstein’s theory of special relativity, which postulates that nothing can travel faster than the speed of light and is demonstrated mathematically by the well-known equation E=mc2.

The ability to instantaneously measure the quantum state of one particle by measuring that of its entangled partner somewhere else in the universe means that that information would have to be delivered faster than lightspeed. This contradicts Einstein’s theory of special relativity. What also remains a mystery is how exactly these particles can interact from such a far distance to share information.

Three decades would pass until another scientist, John Stewart Bell, would develop a method to test the phenomenon, which ultimately enabled later scientists to confirm quantum entanglement.

**Classical vs. Quantum Physics**

If classical physics is life as we know it, the quantum world is like an alternate universe.

Classical physics is the force governing an extremely predictable world, where an apple set on a table stays there until something causes it to move again.

In the quantum world, not only can the apple end up on Mars, but, hypothetically, it could exist both on the table and on Mars at the same time. It could even be inextricably tied to another apple in some other part of the universe through entanglement. Thus, “reality” as we know it is much more uncertain, with the possibility for many solutions or outcomes to exist, rather than just one.

Quantum entanglement remains a spooky part of our world. Check out the resources below to learn more about how NASA scientists are working to unravel the mysteries of our quantum universe.

**Related Resources:**

[SEAQUE (Space Entanglement and Annealing Quantum Experiment)](https://science.nasa.gov/biological-physical/investigations/seaque-space-entanglement-and-annealing-quantum-experiment/)

[NASA Demonstrates ‘Ultra-Cool’ Quantum Sensor for First Time in Space](https://www.jpl.nasa.gov/news/nasa-demonstrates-ultra-cool-quantum-sensor-for-first-time-in-space/)

Title: Quantum entanglement - Wikipedia
[Jump to content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#bodyContent)

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Physics phenomenon

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/SPDC_figure.png/250px-SPDC_figure.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SPDC_figure.png) [Spontaneous parametric down-conversion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spontaneous_parametric_down-conversion "Spontaneous parametric down-conversion") process can split photons into type II photon pairs with mutually perpendicular polarization.

**Quantum entanglement** is the phenomenon wherein the [quantum state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_state "Quantum state") of each [particle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subatomic_particle "Subatomic particle") in a group cannot be described independently of the state of the others, even when the particles are separated by a large distance. The topic of quantum entanglement is at the heart of the disparity between [classical physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_physics "Classical physics") and [quantum physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_physics "Quantum physics"): entanglement is a primary feature of quantum mechanics not present in [classical mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_mechanics "Classical mechanics").[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-horodecki2007-1): 867

[Measurements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measurement#Quantum_mechanics "Measurement") of [physical properties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_properties "Physical properties") such as [position](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Position_(vector) "Position (vector)"), [momentum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Momentum "Momentum"), [spin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_(physics) "Spin (physics)"), and [polarization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polarization_(waves) "Polarization (waves)") performed on entangled particles can, in some cases, be found to be perfectly [correlated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Correlated "Correlated"). For example, if a pair of entangled particles is generated such that their total spin is known to be zero, and one particle is found to have clockwise spin on a first axis, then the spin of the other particle, measured on the same axis, is found to be anticlockwise. This behavior gives rise to seemingly [paradoxical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paradox "Paradox") effects: any measurement of a particle's properties results in an apparent and irreversible [wave function collapse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wave_function_collapse "Wave function collapse") of that particle and changes the original quantum state. With entangled particles, such measurements affect the entangled system as a whole.

Such phenomena were the subject of a 1935 paper by [Albert Einstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Einstein "Albert Einstein"), [Boris Podolsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Podolsky "Boris Podolsky"), and [Nathan Rosen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nathan_Rosen "Nathan Rosen"),[\[2\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Einstein1935-2) and several papers by [Erwin Schrödinger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger "Erwin Schrödinger") shortly thereafter,[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Schr%C3%B6dinger1935-3)[\[4\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Schr%C3%B6dinger1936-4) describing what came to be known as the [EPR paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EPR_paradox "EPR paradox"). Einstein and others considered such behavior impossible, as it violated the [local realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_realism "Local realism") view of [causality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Causality "Causality")[\[5\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-5) and argued that the accepted formulation of [quantum mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanics "Quantum mechanics") must therefore be incomplete.

Later, the counterintuitive predictions of quantum mechanics were verified in tests where polarization or spin of entangled particles were measured at separate locations, statistically violating [Bell's inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell%27s_theorem "Bell's theorem").[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Clauser-6)[\[7\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:0-7)[\[8\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:1-8)[\[9\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:2-9) This established that the correlations produced from quantum entanglement cannot be explained in terms of [local hidden variables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_hidden_variable_theory "Local hidden variable theory"), i.e., properties contained within the individual particles themselves.

Entanglement can produce statistical [correlations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Correlation "Correlation") between events in widely separated places, but it cannot be used for [faster-than-light communication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faster-than-light_communication "Faster-than-light communication").[\[10\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-10)[\[11\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-11)[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Griffiths-12): 453  Quantum entanglement has been demonstrated experimentally with [photons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photon "Photon"),[\[13\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Kocher1-13)[\[14\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Kocherphd-14) [electrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron "Electron"),[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-NTR-20151021-15)[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-NYT-20151021-16) [top quarks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Top_quark "Top quark"),[\[17\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-17) molecules[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-18) and even small [diamonds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diamond "Diamond").[\[19\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-19) The use of quantum entanglement in [communication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_communication "Quantum communication") and [computation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_computing "Quantum computing") is an active area of research and development.

## History

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: History")\]

Background: [History of quantum mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_quantum_mechanics "History of quantum mechanics")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/NYT_May_4%2C_1935.jpg/250px-NYT_May_4%2C_1935.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:NYT_May_4,_1935.jpg) Article headline regarding the [Einstein–Podolsky–Rosen (EPR) paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EPR_paradox "EPR paradox") paper, in the 4 May 1935 issue of _[The New York Times](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_New_York_Times "The New York Times")_

Albert Einstein and Niels Bohr engaged in a long-running collegial dispute over the interpretation of quantum mechanics, now known as the [Bohr–Einstein debates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bohr%E2%80%93Einstein_debates "Bohr–Einstein debates"). During these debates, Einstein introduced a [thought experiment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thought_experiment "Thought experiment") involving a box that emits a photon. He noted that the experimenter's choice of which measurement to make on the box would change what can be predicted about the photon, even when the photon is very far away. This argument, which Einstein had formulated by 1931, was an early recognition of what would later be called entanglement.[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-20) That same year, [Hermann Weyl](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermann_Weyl "Hermann Weyl") observed in his textbook on [group theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Group_theory "Group theory") and quantum mechanics that quantum systems composed of multiple interacting parts exhibit a kind of _[Gestalt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gestalt_psychology "Gestalt psychology"),_ in which "the whole is greater than the sum of its parts".[\[21\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-21)[\[22\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-22) In 1932, [Erwin Schrödinger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger "Erwin Schrödinger") derived the defining equations of quantum entanglement but left them unpublished.[\[23\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-23) In 1935, [Grete Hermann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grete_Hermann "Grete Hermann") studied the mathematics of an electron interacting with a photon and noted the phenomenon that would later be called entanglement.[\[24\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-24) Later that same year, Einstein, [Boris Podolsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Podolsky "Boris Podolsky") and [Nathan Rosen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nathan_Rosen "Nathan Rosen") published a paper on what is now known as the [Einstein–Podolsky–Rosen (EPR) paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Einstein%E2%80%93Podolsky%E2%80%93Rosen_paradox "Einstein–Podolsky–Rosen paradox"), a thought experiment that attempted to show that "the [quantum-mechanical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum-mechanical "Quantum-mechanical") description of physical reality given by wave functions is not complete".[\[2\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Einstein1935-2) Their thought experiment considered two systems that interact and then separate, and they argued that, afterward, quantum mechanics could not describe the two systems individually.

Shortly after this paper appeared, [Erwin Schrödinger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Erwin_Schr%C3%B6dinger "Erwin Schrödinger") wrote a letter to Einstein in [German](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_language "German language") in which he used the word _Verschränkung_ (translated by himself as _entanglement_) to describe situations like that of the EPR scenario.[\[25\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-MK-25) Schrödinger followed up with a full paper defining and discussing the notion of _entanglement_,[\[26\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Schroeder-2017-26) saying "I would not call \[entanglement\] _one_ but rather _the_ characteristic trait of quantum mechanics, the one that enforces its entire departure from [classical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_mechanics "Classical mechanics") lines of thought."[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Schr%C3%B6dinger1935-3)
Like Einstein, Schrödinger was dissatisfied with the concept of entanglement, because it seemed to violate the speed limit on the transmission of information implicit in the [theory of relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theory_of_relativity "Theory of relativity").[\[27\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-27) Einstein later disparaged quantum mechanics for seemingly exhibiting " _spukhafte Fernwirkung_",[\[28\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-spukhafte-28) or " [spooky action at a distance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spooky_action_at_a_distance "Spooky action at a distance")", meaning the acquisition of a value of a property at one location resulting from a measurement at a distant location.[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-MerminMoon-1985-29)

In 1946, [John Archibald Wheeler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Archibald_Wheeler "John Archibald Wheeler") suggested studying the [polarization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polarization_(physics) "Polarization (physics)") of pairs of [gamma-ray](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamma-ray "Gamma-ray") photons produced by electron– [positron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positron "Positron") annihilation.[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-30) [Chien-Shiung Wu](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chien-Shiung_Wu "Chien-Shiung Wu") and I. Shaknov carried out this experiment in 1949,[\[31\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:3-31) thereby demonstrating that the entangled particle pairs considered by EPR could be created in the laboratory.[\[32\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-32) Despite Schrödinger's claim of its importance, little work on entanglement was published for decades after his paper was published.[\[26\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Schroeder-2017-26) In 1964 [John S. Bell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_S._Bell "John S. Bell") demonstrated an upper limit, seen in [Bell's inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell%27s_inequality "Bell's inequality"), regarding the strength of correlations that can be produced in any theory obeying [local realism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_realism "Local realism"), and showed that quantum theory predicts violations of this limit for certain entangled systems.[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:4-33)[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-34): 405  His inequality is experimentally testable, and there have been numerous [relevant experiments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_test_experiments "Bell test experiments"), starting with the pioneering work of [Stuart Freedman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stuart_Freedman "Stuart Freedman") and [John Clauser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Clauser "John Clauser") in 1972,[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Clauser-6) and [Alain Aspect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alain_Aspect "Alain Aspect")'s experiments in 1982.[\[35\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Aspect1982-35)[\[36\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-hanson-36)[\[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-37)

While Bell actively discouraged students from pursuing work like his as too esoteric, after a talk at Oxford a student named [Artur Ekert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artur_Ekert "Artur Ekert") suggested that the violation of a Bell inequality could be used as a resource for communication.[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Gilder2009-38): 315  Ekert followed up by publishing a [quantum key distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_key_distribution "Quantum key distribution") protocol called [E91](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/E91_protocol "E91 protocol") based on it.[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-ekert91-39)[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-horodecki2007-1): 874 In 1992, the entanglement concept was leveraged to propose [quantum teleportation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_teleportation "Quantum teleportation"),[\[40\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-BBCJPW93-40) an effect that was realized experimentally in 1997.[\[41\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-41)[\[42\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Bouwmeester-1997-42)[\[43\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rome1998-43) Beginning in the mid-1990s, [Anton Zeilinger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anton_Zeilinger "Anton Zeilinger") used the generation of entanglement via [parametric down-conversion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spontaneous_parametric_down-conversion "Spontaneous parametric down-conversion") to develop [entanglement swapping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entanglement_swapping "Entanglement swapping")[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Gilder2009-38): 317  and demonstrate [quantum cryptography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_cryptography "Quantum cryptography") with entangled photons.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-44)[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-45) In 2022, the [Nobel Prize in Physics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physics "Nobel Prize in Physics") was awarded to Aspect, Clauser, and Zeilinger "for experiments with entangled photons, establishing the violation of Bell inequalities and pioneering quantum information science".[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-NobelPrize-46)

## Concept

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Concept")\]

### Meaning of entanglement

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Meaning of entanglement")\]

Just as [energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energy "Energy") is a resource that facilitates mechanical operations, entanglement is a resource that facilitates performing tasks that involve communication and computation.[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Nielsen-2010-47): 106 [\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rieffel2011-48): 218 [\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Bengtsson2017-49): 435 [\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Bub2023-50) The mathematical definition of entanglement can be paraphrased as saying that maximal knowledge about the whole of a system does not imply maximal knowledge about the individual parts of that system.[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rau2021-51) If the quantum state that describes a pair of particles is entangled, then the results of measurements upon one half of the pair can be strongly correlated with the results of measurements upon the other. However, entanglement is not the same as "correlation" as understood in classical probability theory and in daily life. Instead, entanglement can be thought of as _potential_ correlation that can be used to generate actual correlation in an appropriate experiment.[\[52\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Fuchs2011-52): 130  The correlations generated from an entangled quantum state cannot in general be replicated by classical probability.[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Holevo2001-53): 33

An example of entanglement is a [subatomic particle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subatomic_particle "Subatomic particle") that [decays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particle_decay "Particle decay") into an entangled pair of other particles. The decay events obey the various [conservation laws](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_laws "Conservation laws"), and as a result, the measurement outcomes of one daughter particle must be highly correlated with the measurement outcomes of the other daughter particle (so that the total momenta, angular momenta, energy, and so forth remains roughly the same before and after this process). For instance, a [spin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_(physics) "Spin (physics)")-zero particle could decay into a pair of spin-1/2 particles. If there is no orbital angular momentum, the total spin angular momentum after this decay must be zero (by the [conservation of angular momentum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_of_angular_momentum "Conservation of angular momentum")). Whenever the first particle is measured to be [spin up](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_(physics)#Direction "Spin (physics)") on some axis, the other, when measured on the same axis, is always found to be [spin down](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_(physics)#Direction "Spin (physics)"). This is called the spin anti-correlated case and the pair is said to be in the [singlet state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Singlet_state "Singlet state"). Perfect anti-correlations like this could be explained by "hidden variables" within the particles. For example, we could hypothesize that the particles are made in pairs such that one carries a value of "up" while the other carries a value of "down". Then, knowing the result of the spin measurement upon one particle, we could predict that the other will have the opposite value. Bell illustrated this with a story about a colleague, Bertlmann, who always wore socks with mismatching colors. "Which colour he will have on a given foot on a given day is quite unpredictable," Bell wrote, but upon observing "that the first sock is pink you can be already sure that the second sock will not be pink."[\[54\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-54) Revealing the remarkable features of quantum entanglement requires considering multiple distinct experiments, such as spin measurements along different axes, and comparing the correlations obtained in these different configurations.[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Zwiebach2022-55): §18.8

Quantum [systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_system "Physical system") can become entangled through various types of interactions. For some ways in which entanglement may be achieved for experimental purposes, see the section below on [methods](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#Methods_of_creating_entanglement). Entanglement is broken when the entangled particles [decohere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_decoherence "Quantum decoherence") through interaction with the environment; for example, when a measurement is made. In more detail, this process involves the particles becoming entangled with the environment, as a consequence of which, the quantum state describing the particles themselves is no longer entangled.[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Peres1993-56): 369 [\[57\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-57) Mathematically, an entangled system can be defined to be one whose quantum state cannot be factored as a product of states of its local constituents; that is to say, they are not individual particles but are an inseparable whole. When entanglement is present, one constituent cannot be fully described without considering the other(s).[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Mermin2007-58): 18–19 [\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Zwiebach2022-55): §1.5  The state of a composite system is always expressible as a sum, or [superposition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_superposition "Quantum superposition"), of products of states of local constituents; it is entangled if this sum cannot be written as a single product term.[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rieffel2011-48): 39

### Paradox

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Paradox")\]

Main article: [EPR paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EPR_paradox "EPR paradox")

The singlet state described above is the basis for one version of the EPR paradox. In this variant, introduced by [David Bohm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Bohm "David Bohm"), a source emits particles and sends them in opposite directions. The state describing each pair is entangled.[\[59\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-59) In the standard textbook presentation of quantum mechanics, performing a spin measurement on one of the particles causes the wave function for the whole pair to [collapse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wave_function_collapse "Wave function collapse") into a state in which each particle has a definite spin (either up or down) along the axis of measurement. The outcome is random, with each possibility having a probability of 50%. However, if both spins are measured along the same axis, they are found to be anti-correlated. This means that the random outcome of the measurement made on one particle seems to have been transmitted to the other, so that it can make the "right choice" when it too is measured.[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Zwiebach2022-55): §18.8 [\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Griffiths-12): 447–448

The distance and timing of the measurements can be chosen so as to make the interval between the two measurements [spacelike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacelike "Spacelike"), hence, any causal effect connecting the events would have to travel faster than light. According to the principles of [special relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_relativity "Special relativity"), it is not possible for any information to travel between two such measuring events. It is not even possible to say which of the measurements came first. For two spacelike separated events _x_ 1 and _x_ 2 there are [inertial frames](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inertial_frame "Inertial frame") in which _x_ 1 is first and others in which _x_ 2 is first. Therefore, the correlation between the two measurements cannot be explained as one measurement determining the other: different observers would disagree about the role of cause and effect.[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-60)

### Failure of local hidden-variable theories

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Failure of local hidden-variable theories")\]

A possible resolution to the paradox is to assume that quantum theory is incomplete, and the result of measurements depends on predetermined " [hidden variables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hidden-variables_theory "Hidden-variables theory")".[\[61\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Gibney2017-61) The state of the particles being measured contains some hidden variables, whose values effectively determine, right from the moment of separation, what the outcomes of the spin measurements are going to be. This would mean that each particle carries all the required information with it, and nothing needs to be transmitted from one particle to the other at the time of measurement. Einstein and others originally believed this was the only way out of the paradox, and the accepted quantum mechanical description (with a random measurement outcome) must be incomplete. [Local hidden variable theories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Local_hidden-variable_theory "Local hidden-variable theory") fail when measurements of the spin of entangled particles along different axes are considered. If a large number of pairs of such measurements are made (on a large number of pairs of entangled particles), then statistically, if the local realist or hidden variables view were correct, the results would always satisfy [Bell's inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell%27s_inequality "Bell's inequality"). A [number of experiments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_test "Bell test") have shown in practice that Bell's inequality is not satisfied.[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Clauser-6)[\[62\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-62)[\[63\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-63)[\[64\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-64) Moreover, when measurements of the entangled particles are made in moving [relativistic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special_relativity "Special relativity") reference frames, in which each measurement (in its own relativistic time frame) occurs before the other, the measurement results remain correlated.[\[65\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-65)[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Gilder2009-38): 321–324

The fundamental issue about measuring spin along different axes is that these measurements cannot have definite values at the same time―they are [incompatible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Incompatible_observables "Incompatible observables") in the sense that these measurements' maximum simultaneous precision is constrained by the [uncertainty principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uncertainty_principle "Uncertainty principle"). This is contrary to what is found in classical physics, where any number of properties can be measured simultaneously with arbitrary accuracy. It has been proven mathematically that compatible measurements cannot show Bell-inequality-violating correlations,[\[66\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-66) and thus entanglement is a fundamentally non-classical phenomenon.

## Nonlocality and entanglement

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: Nonlocality and entanglement")\]

As discussed above, entanglement is necessary to produce a violation of a [Bell inequality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell%27s_theorem "Bell's theorem"). However, the mere presence of entanglement alone is insufficient,[\[67\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Brunner-RMP2014-67) as Bell himself noted in his 1964 paper.[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:4-33) This is demonstrated, for example, by [Werner states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Werner_state "Werner state"), which are a family of states describing pairs of particles. For appropriate choices of the key parameter that identifies a given Werner state within the full set thereof, the Werner states exhibit entanglement. Yet pairs of particles described by Werner states always admit a local hidden variable model. In other words, these states cannot power the violation of a Bell inequality, despite possessing entanglement.[\[68\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-werner1989-68) This can be generalized from pairs of particles to larger collections as well.[\[69\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Augusiak2015-69)

The violation of Bell inequalities is often called _[quantum nonlocality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_nonlocality "Quantum nonlocality")._ This term is not without controversy.[\[70\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-70) It is sometimes argued that using the term _nonlocality_ carries the unwarranted implication that the violation of Bell inequalities must be explained by physical, faster-than-light signals.[\[71\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Scarani-71) In other words, the failure of local hidden-variable models to reproduce quantum mechanics is not necessarily a sign of true nonlocality in quantum mechanics itself.[\[72\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-72)[\[73\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-73)[\[74\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-74) Despite these reservations, the term _nonlocality_ has become a widespread convention.[\[71\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Scarani-71)

The term _nonlocality_ is sometimes applied to other concepts besides the nonexistence of a local hidden-variable model, such as [whether states can be distinguished by local measurements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unextendible_product_basis "Unextendible product basis").[\[75\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-75) Moreover, [quantum field theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_field_theory "Quantum field theory") is often said to be _local_ because [observables](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observable "Observable") defined within spacetime regions that are [spacelike](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacelike "Spacelike") separated must commute.[\[67\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Brunner-RMP2014-67)[\[76\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-76) These other uses of _local_ and _nonlocal_ are not discussed further here.

## Mathematical details

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: Mathematical details")\]

The following subsections use the formalism and theoretical framework developed in the articles [bra–ket notation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bra%E2%80%93ket_notation "Bra–ket notation") and [mathematical formulation of quantum mechanics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_formulation_of_quantum_mechanics "Mathematical formulation of quantum mechanics").

### Pure states

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: Pure states")\]

Consider two arbitrary quantum systems A and B, with respective [Hilbert spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_space "Hilbert space") HA and HB. The Hilbert space of the composite system is the [tensor product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tensor_product "Tensor product")

HA⊗HB.{\\displaystyle H\_{A}\\otimes H\_{B}.}![{\displaystyle H_{A}\otimes H_{B}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/19fca01f34c847473055f02b00ae2c3e51409901)

If the first system is in state \|ψ⟩A{\\displaystyle \|\\psi \\rangle \_{A}}![{\displaystyle |\psi \rangle _{A}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/435c58e1961f1e68f74f8734418e7c386efd5ef4) and the second in state \|ϕ⟩B{\\displaystyle \|\\phi \\rangle \_{B}}![{\displaystyle |\phi \rangle _{B}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2be1ace3f2710536cfa89e755db7a9f4f9f5136e), the state of the composite system is

\|ψ⟩A⊗\|ϕ⟩B.{\\displaystyle \|\\psi \\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|\\phi \\rangle \_{B}.}![{\displaystyle |\psi \rangle _{A}\otimes |\phi \rangle _{B}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c828da659b247951f5792107fc723ebe7c5577e9)

States of the composite system that can be represented in this form are called separable states, or [product states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Product_state "Product state"). However, not all states of the composite system are separable. Fix a [basis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basis_(linear_algebra) "Basis (linear algebra)"){\|i⟩A}{\\displaystyle \\{\|i\\rangle \_{A}\\}}![{\displaystyle \{|i\rangle _{A}\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3744b6c619227428bd614c13bb9aac750b0e82fe) for HA and a basis {\|j⟩B}{\\displaystyle \\{\|j\\rangle \_{B}\\}}![{\displaystyle \{|j\rangle _{B}\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c4e8a89a80bddaf7a469e3ecc3ec555eede209a7) for HB. The most general state in _HA_ ⊗ _HB_ is of the form

\|ψ⟩AB=∑i,jcij\|i⟩A⊗\|j⟩B{\\displaystyle \|\\psi \\rangle \_{AB}=\\sum \_{i,j}c\_{ij}\|i\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|j\\rangle \_{B}}![{\displaystyle |\psi \rangle _{AB}=\sum _{i,j}c_{ij}|i\rangle _{A}\otimes |j\rangle _{B}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d211d5745f4b52151d8489776dfbdf1fee13dc1f).

This state is separable if there exist vectors \[ciA\],\[cjB\]{\\displaystyle \[c\_{i}^{A}\],\[c\_{j}^{B}\]}![{\displaystyle [c_{i}^{A}],[c_{j}^{B}]}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a2b47f980dac5bc92f73609aef7a6280f2f2be7f) so that cij=ciAcjB,{\\displaystyle c\_{ij}=c\_{i}^{A}c\_{j}^{B},}![{\displaystyle c_{ij}=c_{i}^{A}c_{j}^{B},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/febfdcca5d8a1c72ab47af48880d828bb7d60aab) yielding \|ψ⟩A=∑iciA\|i⟩A{\\textstyle \|\\psi \\rangle \_{A}=\\sum \_{i}c\_{i}^{A}\|i\\rangle \_{A}}![{\textstyle |\psi \rangle _{A}=\sum _{i}c_{i}^{A}|i\rangle _{A}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7f0bbef833d99d88a2228377927c3b0c01229d78) and \|ϕ⟩B=∑jcjB\|j⟩B.{\\textstyle \|\\phi \\rangle \_{B}=\\sum \_{j}c\_{j}^{B}\|j\\rangle \_{B}.}![{\textstyle |\phi \rangle _{B}=\sum _{j}c_{j}^{B}|j\rangle _{B}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d82f19111902fc3a2c36f633c6925a9314f48d72) It is inseparable if for any vectors \[ciA\],\[cjB\]{\\displaystyle \[c\_{i}^{A}\],\[c\_{j}^{B}\]}![{\displaystyle [c_{i}^{A}],[c_{j}^{B}]}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a2b47f980dac5bc92f73609aef7a6280f2f2be7f) at least for one pair of coordinates ciA,cjB{\\displaystyle c\_{i}^{A},c\_{j}^{B}}![{\displaystyle c_{i}^{A},c_{j}^{B}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/79bd35827efb95e0fcfebd87e1b5071ecc50709f) we have cij≠ciAcjB.{\\displaystyle c\_{ij}\\neq c\_{i}^{A}c\_{j}^{B}.}![{\displaystyle c_{ij}\neq c_{i}^{A}c_{j}^{B}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/edc74f42a3e75058769623c147eb649b8e284d1c) If a state is inseparable, it is called an 'entangled state'.[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rieffel2011-48): 218 [\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Zwiebach2022-55): §1.5

For example, given two basis vectors {\|0⟩A,\|1⟩A}{\\displaystyle \\{\|0\\rangle \_{A},\|1\\rangle \_{A}\\}}![{\displaystyle \{|0\rangle _{A},|1\rangle _{A}\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/29ccb462de8b00e179e027c5d0834ac3691bc45b) of HA and two basis vectors {\|0⟩B,\|1⟩B}{\\displaystyle \\{\|0\\rangle \_{B},\|1\\rangle \_{B}\\}}![{\displaystyle \{|0\rangle _{B},|1\rangle _{B}\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/357e2a7e30db6187849c070e3398ff8d031d1178) of HB, the following is an entangled state:

12(\|0⟩A⊗\|1⟩B−\|1⟩A⊗\|0⟩B).{\\displaystyle {\\tfrac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}\\left(\|0\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|1\\rangle \_{B}-\|1\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|0\\rangle \_{B}\\right).}![{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{\sqrt {2}}}\left(|0\rangle _{A}\otimes |1\rangle _{B}-|1\rangle _{A}\otimes |0\rangle _{B}\right).}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4053aa0fcea6250c60262618348a170fc18ac03b)

If the composite system is in this state, it is impossible to attribute to either system A or system B a definite [pure state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pure_state "Pure state"). Another way to say this is that while the [von Neumann entropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_entropy "Von Neumann entropy") of the whole state is zero (as it is for any pure state), the entropy of the subsystems is greater than zero. In this sense, the systems are "entangled". The above example is one of four [Bell states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_states "Bell states"), which are (maximally) entangled pure states (pure states of the _HA_ ⊗ _HB_ space, but which cannot be separated into pure states of each HA and HB).[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Zwiebach2022-55): §18.6

Now suppose Alice is an observer for system A, and Bob is an observer for system B. If in the entangled state given above Alice makes a measurement in the {\|0⟩,\|1⟩}{\\displaystyle \\{\|0\\rangle ,\|1\\rangle \\}}![{\displaystyle \{|0\rangle ,|1\rangle \}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7a72099dd8347d05e92c98f19db855b1651f8402) eigenbasis of A, there are two possible outcomes, occurring with equal probability: Alice can obtain the outcome 0, or she can obtain the outcome 1. If she obtains the outcome 0, then she can predict with certainty that Bob's result will be 1. Likewise, if she obtains the outcome 1, then she can predict with certainty that Bob's result will be 0. In other words, the results of measurements on the two [qubits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qubit "Qubit") will be perfectly anti-correlated. This remains true even if the systems A and B are spatially separated. This is the foundation of the EPR paradox.[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Nielsen-2010-47): 113–114  The outcome of Alice's measurement is random. Alice cannot decide which state to collapse the composite system into, and therefore cannot transmit information to Bob by acting on her system. Causality is thus preserved, in this particular scheme. For the general argument, see [no-communication theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/No-communication_theorem "No-communication theorem").

### Ensembles

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: Ensembles")\]

As mentioned above, a state of a quantum system is given by a unit vector in a Hilbert space. More generally, if one has less information about the system, then one calls it an 'ensemble' and describes it by a [density matrix](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Density_matrix "Density matrix"), which is a [positive-semidefinite matrix](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive-semidefinite_matrix "Positive-semidefinite matrix"), or a [trace class](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trace_class "Trace class") when the state space is infinite-dimensional, and which has trace 1. By the [spectral theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectral_theorem "Spectral theorem"), such a matrix takes the general form:

ρ=∑iwi\|αi⟩⟨αi\|,{\\displaystyle \\rho =\\sum \_{i}w\_{i}\|\\alpha \_{i}\\rangle \\langle \\alpha \_{i}\|,}![{\displaystyle \rho =\sum _{i}w_{i}|\alpha _{i}\rangle \langle \alpha _{i}|,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6b4708b78f8713fd851ab3f5d8e97d8f85b873c0)

where the _w_ _i_ are positive-valued probabilities (they sum up to 1), the vectors _α_ _i_ are unit vectors, and in the infinite-dimensional case, we would take the closure of such states in the trace norm. We can interpret ρ as representing an ensemble where wi{\\displaystyle w\_{i}}![{\displaystyle w_{i}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe22f0329d3ecb2e1880d44d191aba0e5475db68) is the proportion of the ensemble whose states are \|αi⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\alpha \_{i}\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\alpha _{i}\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0894b0cce03f69124a81e6f240e13af70918a098). When a mixed state has rank 1, it therefore describes a 'pure ensemble'. When there is less than total information about the state of a quantum system we need [density matrices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#Reduced_density_matrices) to represent the state.[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Peres1993-56): 73–74 [\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Holevo2001-53): 13–15 [\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Zwiebach2022-55): §22.2

Experimentally, a mixed ensemble might be realized as follows. Consider a "black box" apparatus that spits [electrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron "Electron") towards an observer. The electrons' Hilbert spaces are [identical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indistinguishable_particles "Indistinguishable particles"). The apparatus might produce electrons that are all in the same state; in this case, the electrons received by the observer are then a pure ensemble. However, the apparatus could produce electrons in different states. For example, it could produce two populations of electrons: one with state \|z+⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\mathbf {z} +\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\mathbf {z} +\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/567f15dd6efff02e103e9637ee7cf4954ff92662) with spins aligned in the positive **z** direction, and the other with state \|y−⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\mathbf {y} -\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\mathbf {y} -\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ee2032e156f9a7256cfb8095258bce23a33e0345) with spins aligned in the negative **y** direction. Generally, this is a mixed ensemble, as there can be any number of populations, each corresponding to a different state. Following the definition above, for a bipartite composite system, mixed states are just density matrices on _HA_ ⊗ _HB_. That is, it has the general form

ρ=∑iwi\[∑jc¯ij(\|αij⟩⊗\|βij⟩)\]\[∑kcik(⟨αik\|⊗⟨βik\|)\]{\\displaystyle \\rho =\\sum \_{i}w\_{i}\\left\[\\sum \_{j}{\\bar {c}}\_{ij}(\|\\alpha \_{ij}\\rangle \\otimes \|\\beta \_{ij}\\rangle )\\right\]\\left\[\\sum \_{k}c\_{ik}(\\langle \\alpha \_{ik}\|\\otimes \\langle \\beta \_{ik}\|)\\right\]}![{\displaystyle \rho =\sum _{i}w_{i}\left[\sum _{j}{\bar {c}}_{ij}(|\alpha _{ij}\rangle \otimes |\beta _{ij}\rangle )\right]\left[\sum _{k}c_{ik}(\langle \alpha _{ik}|\otimes \langle \beta _{ik}|)\right]}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cbf2f7c56adc4e273d3e3c73ee6a66e190c3f35c)

where the _w_ _i_ are positively valued probabilities, ∑j\|cij\|2=1{\\textstyle \\sum \_{j}\|c\_{ij}\|^{2}=1}![{\textstyle \sum _{j}|c_{ij}|^{2}=1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/407c8b79636e8aafbad96aa6aaaeeefd52d73008), and the vectors are unit vectors. This is self-adjoint and positive and has trace 1.

Extending the definition of separability from the pure case, we say that a mixed state is separable if it can be written as[\[77\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Laloe-77): 131–132

ρ=∑iwiρiA⊗ρiB,{\\displaystyle \\rho =\\sum \_{i}w\_{i}\\rho \_{i}^{A}\\otimes \\rho \_{i}^{B},}![{\displaystyle \rho =\sum _{i}w_{i}\rho _{i}^{A}\otimes \rho _{i}^{B},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6597264612d7732f42fd586b145e28e9b04aa329)

where the _w_ _i_ are positively valued probabilities and the ρiA{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{i}^{A}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{i}^{A}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/60b5bc5b7612e68b45a4ce589c0e4c289df55b34)s and ρiB{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{i}^{B}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{i}^{B}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/475221b5b58c88f20750357c28e1c45b9087f9bf)s are themselves mixed states (density operators) on the subsystems A and B respectively. In other words, a state is separable if it is a probability distribution over uncorrelated states, or product states. By writing the density matrices as sums of pure ensembles and expanding, we may assume without loss of generality that ρiA{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{i}^{A}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{i}^{A}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/60b5bc5b7612e68b45a4ce589c0e4c289df55b34) and ρiB{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{i}^{B}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{i}^{B}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/475221b5b58c88f20750357c28e1c45b9087f9bf) are themselves pure ensembles. A state is then said to be entangled if it is not separable. In general, finding out whether or not a mixed state is entangled is considered difficult. The general bipartite case has been shown to be [NP-hard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NP-hardness "NP-hardness").[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-78) For the 2 × 2 and 2 × 3 cases, a necessary and sufficient criterion for separability is given by the famous [Positive Partial Transpose](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive_Partial_Transpose "Positive Partial Transpose") (PPT) condition.[\[79\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-79)

### Reduced density matrices

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Reduced density matrices")\]

The idea of a reduced density matrix was introduced by [Paul Dirac](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paul_Dirac "Paul Dirac") in 1930.[\[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Dirac1930-80) Consider as above systems A and B each with a Hilbert space HA, HB. Let the state of the composite system be

\|Ψ⟩∈HA⊗HB.{\\displaystyle \|\\Psi \\rangle \\in H\_{A}\\otimes H\_{B}.}![{\displaystyle |\Psi \rangle \in H_{A}\otimes H_{B}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b222fc006a0cca79472f2fbb1313f03c2b512490)

As indicated above, in general there is no way to associate a pure state to the component system A. However, it still is possible to associate a density matrix. Let

ρT=\|Ψ⟩⟨Ψ\|{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{T}=\|\\Psi \\rangle \\;\\langle \\Psi \|}![{\displaystyle \rho _{T}=|\Psi \rangle \;\langle \Psi |}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/354e47cf72ebfb19333d551ac631ca85d9fc2c0b).

which is the [projection operator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projection_operator "Projection operator") onto this state. The state of A is the [partial trace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partial_trace "Partial trace") of ρT over the basis of system B:

ρA=def∑jNB(IA⊗⟨j\|B)(\|Ψ⟩⟨Ψ\|)(IA⊗\|j⟩B)=TrBρT.{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{A}\ {\\stackrel {\\mathrm {def} }{=}}\ \\sum \_{j}^{N\_{B}}\\left(I\_{A}\\otimes \\langle j\|\_{B}\\right)\\left(\|\\Psi \\rangle \\langle \\Psi \|\\right)\\left(I\_{A}\\otimes \|j\\rangle \_{B}\\right)={\\hbox{Tr}}\_{B}\\;\\rho \_{T}.}![{\displaystyle \rho _{A}\ {\stackrel {\mathrm {def} }{=}}\ \sum _{j}^{N_{B}}\left(I_{A}\otimes \langle j|_{B}\right)\left(|\Psi \rangle \langle \Psi |\right)\left(I_{A}\otimes |j\rangle _{B}\right)={\hbox{Tr}}_{B}\;\rho _{T}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e603e36b10b46642f2ab810508663f112c64b376)

The sum occurs over NB:=dim⁡(HB){\\displaystyle N\_{B}:=\\dim(H\_{B})}![{\displaystyle N_{B}:=\dim(H_{B})}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cc45ee38d11c298d2c78941259ec8477b0aee5cb) and IA{\\displaystyle I\_{A}}![{\displaystyle I_{A}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fad11afeef762471a89c8a258282a40af27d6516) the identity operator in HA{\\displaystyle H\_{A}}![{\displaystyle H_{A}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b3f561a051435337d6fd20d1b3a2cb945fe97f3b). ρA is sometimes called the reduced density matrix of ρ on subsystem A. Colloquially, we "trace out" or "trace over" system B to obtain the reduced density matrix on A.[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rieffel2011-48): 207–212 [\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rau2021-51): 133 [\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Zwiebach2022-55): §22.4

For example, the reduced density matrix of A for the entangled state

12(\|0⟩A⊗\|1⟩B−\|1⟩A⊗\|0⟩B),{\\displaystyle {\\tfrac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}\\left(\|0\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|1\\rangle \_{B}-\|1\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|0\\rangle \_{B}\\right),}![{\displaystyle {\tfrac {1}{\sqrt {2}}}\left(|0\rangle _{A}\otimes |1\rangle _{B}-|1\rangle _{A}\otimes |0\rangle _{B}\right),}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0a819774559f3e64569ce18b2c7de993d2066d24)

discussed above is[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Zwiebach2022-55): §22.4

ρA=12(\|0⟩A⟨0\|A+\|1⟩A⟨1\|A).{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{A}={\\tfrac {1}{2}}\\left(\|0\\rangle \_{A}\\langle 0\|\_{A}+\|1\\rangle \_{A}\\langle 1\|\_{A}\\right).}![{\displaystyle \rho _{A}={\tfrac {1}{2}}\left(|0\rangle _{A}\langle 0|_{A}+|1\rangle _{A}\langle 1|_{A}\right).}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/96d96b1e42a9c740ebe571acad39d0844a8473c8)

This demonstrates that the reduced density matrix for an entangled pure ensemble is a mixed ensemble. In contrast, the density matrix of A for the pure product state \|ψ⟩A⊗\|ϕ⟩B{\\displaystyle \|\\psi \\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|\\phi \\rangle \_{B}}![{\displaystyle |\psi \rangle _{A}\otimes |\phi \rangle _{B}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3ff74a24657c12842e203b89a4427f0cfabd067d) discussed above is[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Nielsen-2010-47): 106

ρA=\|ψ⟩A⟨ψ\|A,{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{A}=\|\\psi \\rangle \_{A}\\langle \\psi \|\_{A},}![{\displaystyle \rho _{A}=|\psi \rangle _{A}\langle \psi |_{A},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5ee961e82ef88b7cf94c6214e0eda56eed6f61c1)

the projection operator onto \|ψ⟩A{\\displaystyle \|\\psi \\rangle \_{A}}![{\displaystyle |\psi \rangle _{A}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/435c58e1961f1e68f74f8734418e7c386efd5ef4).

In general, a bipartite pure state _ρ_ is entangled if and only if its reduced states are mixed rather than pure.[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rau2021-51): 131

### Entanglement as a resource

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: Entanglement as a resource")\]

In quantum information theory, entangled states are considered a 'resource', i.e., something costly to produce and that allows implementing valuable transformations.[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Chitambar2019-81)[\[82\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-GG-2022-82) The setting in which this perspective is most evident is that of "distant labs", i.e., two quantum systems labelled "A" and "B" on each of which arbitrary [quantum operations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_operation "Quantum operation") can be performed, but which do not interact with each other quantum mechanically. The only interaction allowed is the exchange of classical information, which combined with the most general local quantum operations gives rise to the class of operations called [LOCC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LOCC "LOCC") (local operations and classical communication). These operations do not allow the production of entangled states between systems A and B. But if A and B are provided with a supply of entangled states, then these, together with LOCC operations can enable a larger class of transformations.

If Alice and Bob share an entangled state, Alice can tell Bob over a telephone call how to reproduce a quantum state \|Ψ⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\Psi \\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\Psi \rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e77f6b1e903837c5765c9683da41dd93199621c) she has in her lab. Alice performs a joint measurement on \|Ψ⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\Psi \\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\Psi \rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e77f6b1e903837c5765c9683da41dd93199621c) together with her half of the entangled state and tells Bob the results. Using Alice's results Bob operates on his half of the entangled state to make it equal to \|Ψ⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\Psi \\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\Psi \rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e77f6b1e903837c5765c9683da41dd93199621c). Since Alice's measurement necessarily erases the quantum state of the system in her lab, the state \|Ψ⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\Psi \\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\Psi \rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e77f6b1e903837c5765c9683da41dd93199621c) is not copied, but transferred: it is said to be " [teleported](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_teleportation "Quantum teleportation")" to Bob's laboratory through this protocol.[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Nielsen-2010-47): 27 [\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-horodecki2007-1): 875 [\[83\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-83)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Entanglement_swapping.svg/250px-Entanglement_swapping.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Entanglement_swapping.svg) Entanglement of states from independent sources can be swapped through Bell state measurement.[\[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-GuoReview2023-84): 341

[Entanglement swapping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entanglement_swapping "Entanglement swapping") is variant of teleportation that allows two parties that have never interacted to share an entangled state. The swapping protocol begins with two EPR sources. One source emits an entangled pair of particles A and B, while the other emits a second entangled pair of particles C and D. Particles B and C are subjected to a measurement in the basis of Bell states. The state of the remaining particles, A and D, collapses to a Bell state, leaving them entangled despite never having interacted with each other.[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-horodecki2007-1)[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Pan1998-85)

An interaction between a qubit of A and a qubit of B can be realized by first teleporting A's qubit to B, then letting it interact with B's qubit (which is now a LOCC operation, since both qubits are in B's lab) and then teleporting the qubit back to A. Two maximally entangled states of two qubits are used up in this process. Thus entangled states are a resource that enables the realization of quantum interactions (or of quantum channels) in a setting where only LOCC are available, but they are consumed in the process. There are other applications where entanglement can be seen as a resource, e.g., private communication or distinguishing quantum states.[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-horodecki2007-1)

### Multipartite entanglement

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Multipartite entanglement")\]

Main article: [Multipartite entanglement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multipartite_entanglement "Multipartite entanglement")

Quantum states describing systems made of more than two pieces can also be entangled. An example for a three-qubit system is the [Greenberger–Horne–Zeilinger (GHZ) state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenberger%E2%80%93Horne%E2%80%93Zeilinger_state "Greenberger–Horne–Zeilinger state"),
\|GHZ⟩=\|000⟩+\|111⟩2.{\\displaystyle \|\\mathrm {GHZ} \\rangle ={\\frac {\|000\\rangle +\|111\\rangle }{\\sqrt {2}}}.}![{\displaystyle |\mathrm {GHZ} \rangle ={\frac {|000\rangle +|111\rangle }{\sqrt {2}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/806963b685467e9f5252c8199a6946394a43f460)
Another three-qubit example is the [W state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/W_state "W state"):
\|W⟩=\|001⟩+\|010⟩+\|100⟩3.{\\displaystyle \|\\mathrm {W} \\rangle ={\\frac {\|001\\rangle +\|010\\rangle +\|100\\rangle }{\\sqrt {3}}}.}![{\displaystyle |\mathrm {W} \rangle ={\frac {|001\rangle +|010\rangle +|100\rangle }{\sqrt {3}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/532e85aa6d0ab1895ef5e81c257ed75f9c7d8d34)
Tracing out any one of the three qubits turns the GHZ state into a separable state, whereas the result of tracing over any of the three qubits in the W state is still entangled. This illustrates how multipartite entanglement is a more complicated topic than bipartite entanglement: systems composed of three or more parts can exhibit multiple qualitatively different types of entanglement.[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Bengtsson2017-49): 493–497  A single particle cannot be maximally entangled with more than a particle at a time, a property called [monogamy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monogamy_of_entanglement "Monogamy of entanglement").[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-86)

### Classification of entanglement

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Classification of entanglement")\]

Not all quantum states are equally valuable as a resource. One method to quantify this value is to use an [entanglement measure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#Entanglement_measures) that assigns a numerical value to each quantum state. However, it is often interesting to settle for a coarser way to compare quantum states. This gives rise to different classification schemes. Most entanglement classes are defined based on whether states can be converted to other states using LOCC or a subclass of these operations. The smaller the set of allowed operations, the finer the classification. Important examples are:

- If two states can be transformed into each other by a local unitary operation, they are said to be in the same _LU class_. This is the finest of the usually considered classes. Two states in the same LU class have the same value for entanglement measures and the same value as a resource in the distant-labs setting. There is an infinite number of different LU classes (even in the simplest case of two qubits in a pure state).[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-GRB1998-87)[\[88\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Kraus2010-88)
- If two states can be transformed into each other by local operations including measurements with probability larger than 0, they are said to be in the same 'SLOCC class' ("stochastic LOCC"). Qualitatively, two states ρ1{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{1}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{1}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e0f2d347f2a0ed7f7c9808c427a89813b957017) and ρ2{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{2}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/793b211571b3ffe34c4639654d567296d29d7f72) in the same SLOCC class are equally powerful, since one can transform each into the other, but since the transformations ρ1→ρ2{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{1}\\to \\rho \_{2}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{1}\to \rho _{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/942457dace61f7de964e603f92a3f2f16138d687) and ρ2→ρ1{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{2}\\to \\rho \_{1}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{2}\to \rho _{1}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7190e9057b105b0847bbb547e18f29bb647b6996) may succeed with different probability, they are no longer equally valuable.
