E.g., for two pure qubits there are only two SLOCC classes: the entangled states (which contains both the (maximally entangled) Bell states and weakly entangled states like \|00⟩+0.01\|11⟩{\\displaystyle \|00\\rangle +0.01\|11\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |00\rangle +0.01|11\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/587cf78fb2f0efce5b5bc27af0fa8fe01d95bf25)) and the separable ones (i.e., product states like \|00⟩{\\displaystyle \|00\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |00\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/79016644d7bb5d4282f69bf8af1befa3131445fb)).[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-89)[\[90\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-GoWa2010-90)
- Instead of considering transformations of single copies of a state (like ρ1→ρ2{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{1}\\to \\rho \_{2}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{1}\to \rho _{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/942457dace61f7de964e603f92a3f2f16138d687)) one can define classes based on the possibility of multi-copy transformations. E.g., there are examples when ρ1→ρ2{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{1}\\to \\rho \_{2}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{1}\to \rho _{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/942457dace61f7de964e603f92a3f2f16138d687) is impossible by LOCC, but ρ1⊗ρ1→ρ2{\\displaystyle \\rho \_{1}\\otimes \\rho \_{1}\\to \\rho \_{2}}![{\displaystyle \rho _{1}\otimes \rho _{1}\to \rho _{2}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/18f007d3601506321810c4cea20dd31586650876) is possible. A very important (and very coarse) classification is based on the property whether it is possible to transform an arbitrarily large number of copies of a state ρ{\\displaystyle \\rho }![{\displaystyle \rho }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1f7d439671d1289b6a816e6af7a304be40608d64) into at least one pure entangled state. States that have this property are called [distillable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entanglement_distillation "Entanglement distillation"). These states are the most useful quantum states since, given enough of them, they can be transformed (with local operations) into any entangled state and hence allow for all possible uses. It came initially as a surprise that not all entangled states are distillable; those that are not are called " [bound entangled](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bound_entanglement "Bound entanglement")".[\[91\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-HHH97-91)[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-horodecki2007-1)

A different entanglement classification is based on what the quantum correlations present in a state allow A and B to do: one distinguishes three subsets of entangled states: (1) the _[non-local](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_nonlocality "Quantum nonlocality") states_, which produce correlations that cannot be explained by a local hidden variable model and thus violate a Bell inequality, (2) the _[steerable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_steering "Quantum steering") states_ that contain sufficient correlations for A to modify ("steer") by local measurements the conditional reduced state of B in such a way, that A can prove to B that the state they possess is indeed entangled, and finally (3) those entangled states that are neither non-local nor steerable. All three sets are non-empty.[\[92\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-WJD2007-92)

### Entropy

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Entropy")\]

In this section, the entropy of a mixed state is discussed as well as how it can be viewed as a measure of quantum entanglement.

#### Definition

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Definition")\]

In classical [information theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_theory "Information theory") H, the [Shannon entropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shannon_entropy "Shannon entropy"), is associated to a probability distribution, p1,⋯,pn{\\displaystyle p\_{1},\\cdots ,p\_{n}}![{\displaystyle p_{1},\cdots ,p_{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3711f3a8dc0f30eca8b99ff7fb660d911007ab7a), in the following way:[\[93\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-SE-93)

H(p1,⋯,pn)=−∑ipilog2⁡pi.{\\displaystyle H(p\_{1},\\cdots ,p\_{n})=-\\sum \_{i}p\_{i}\\log \_{2}p\_{i}.}![{\displaystyle H(p_{1},\cdots ,p_{n})=-\sum _{i}p_{i}\log _{2}p_{i}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/875a53f0d12455b69f2b0ee8230fc98e151bc87a)

Since a mixed state ρ is a probability distribution over an ensemble, this leads naturally to the definition of the [von Neumann entropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Von_Neumann_entropy "Von Neumann entropy"):[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Peres1993-56): 264

S(ρ)=−Tr(ρlog2⁡ρ),{\\displaystyle S(\\rho )=-{\\hbox{Tr}}\\left(\\rho \\log \_{2}{\\rho }\\right),}![{\displaystyle S(\rho )=-{\hbox{Tr}}\left(\rho \log _{2}{\rho }\right),}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e68e4845f55bd5775f163c25624fb141d1d1330)

which can be expressed in terms of the [eigenvalues](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eigenvalue "Eigenvalue") of ρ:

S(ρ)=−Tr(ρlog2⁡ρ)=−∑iλilog2⁡λi{\\displaystyle S(\\rho )=-{\\hbox{Tr}}\\left(\\rho \\log \_{2}{\\rho }\\right)=-\\sum \_{i}\\lambda \_{i}\\log \_{2}\\lambda \_{i}}![{\displaystyle S(\rho )=-{\hbox{Tr}}\left(\rho \log _{2}{\rho }\right)=-\sum _{i}\lambda _{i}\log _{2}\lambda _{i}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/928c2d6ade5615fad5fe82e67e17ca9b07640bb4).

Since an event of probability 0 should not contribute to the entropy, and given that

limp→0plog⁡p=0,{\\displaystyle \\lim \_{p\\to 0}p\\log p=0,}![{\displaystyle \lim _{p\to 0}p\log p=0,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1e4050c3b4a3183e1f8af387f25474c7e83bd980)

the convention 0 log(0) = 0 is adopted. When a pair of particles is described by the spin singlet state discussed above, the von Neumann entropy of either particle is log(2), which can be shown to be the maximum entropy for 2 × 2 mixed states.[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Holevo2001-53): 15

#### As a measure of entanglement

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=16 "Edit section: As a measure of entanglement")\]

Entropy provides one tool that can be used to quantify entanglement, although other entanglement measures exist.[\[94\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Plenio-94)[\[95\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Vedral2002-95) If the overall system is pure, the entropy of one subsystem can be used to measure its degree of entanglement with the other subsystems. For bipartite pure states, the von Neumann entropy of reduced states is the unique measure of entanglement in the sense that it is the only function on the family of states that satisfies certain axioms required of an entanglement measure.[\[96\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-96) It is a classical result that the Shannon entropy achieves its maximum at, and only at, the uniform probability distribution {1/ _n_, ..., 1/ _n_}.[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Nielsen-2010-47): 505  Therefore, a bipartite pure state _ρ_ ∈ _H_ A ⊗ _H_ B is said to be a _maximally entangled state_ if the reduced state of each subsystem of ρ is the diagonal matrix[\[97\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-97)

\[1n⋱1n\].{\\displaystyle {\\begin{bmatrix}{\\frac {1}{n}}&&\\\&\\ddots &\\\&&{\\frac {1}{n}}\\end{bmatrix}}.}![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}{\frac {1}{n}}&&\\&\ddots &\\&&{\frac {1}{n}}\end{bmatrix}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0ac2c4a2dbfa47eec57c86a3f4939f6aaf0adc23)

For mixed states, the reduced von Neumann entropy is not the only reasonable entanglement measure.[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Bengtsson2017-49): 471 [Rényi entropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R%C3%A9nyi_entropy "Rényi entropy") can be used as a measure of entanglement.[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Bengtsson2017-49): 447, 480 [\[98\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-98)

### Entanglement measures

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Entanglement measures")\]

Entanglement measures quantify the amount of entanglement in a (often viewed as a bipartite) quantum state. As aforementioned, [entanglement entropy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entropy_of_entanglement "Entropy of entanglement") is the standard measure of entanglement for pure states (but no longer a measure of entanglement for mixed states). For mixed states, there are some entanglement measures in the literature[\[94\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Plenio-94) and no single one is standard.

- Entanglement cost
- [Distillable entanglement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entanglement_distillation "Entanglement distillation")
- [Entanglement of formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entanglement_of_formation "Entanglement of formation")
- [Concurrence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concurrence_(quantum_computing) "Concurrence (quantum computing)")
- [Relative entropy of entanglement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_relative_entropy "Quantum relative entropy")
- [Squashed entanglement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squashed_entanglement "Squashed entanglement")
- [Logarithmic negativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Negativity_(quantum_mechanics)#Logarithmic_negativity "Negativity (quantum mechanics)")

Most (but not all) of these entanglement measures reduce for pure states to entanglement entropy, and are difficult ( [NP-hard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NP-hard "NP-hard")) to compute for mixed states as the dimension of the entangled system grows.[\[99\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-99)

### Quantum field theory

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Quantum field theory")\]

The [Reeh–Schlieder theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reeh%E2%80%93Schlieder_theorem "Reeh–Schlieder theorem") of [quantum field theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_field_theory "Quantum field theory") is sometimes interpreted as saying that entanglement is omnipresent in the [quantum vacuum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_vacuum "Quantum vacuum").[\[100\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-100)

## Applications

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Applications")\]

Entanglement has many applications in [quantum information theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_information_theory "Quantum information theory"). With the aid of entanglement, otherwise impossible tasks may be achieved. Among the best-known applications of entanglement are [superdense coding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superdense_coding "Superdense coding") and quantum teleportation.[\[42\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Bouwmeester-1997-42) Most researchers believe that entanglement is necessary to realize [quantum computing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_computer "Quantum computer") (although this is disputed by some).[\[101\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-jozsa02-101) Entanglement is used in some protocols of [quantum cryptography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_cryptography "Quantum cryptography"),[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-ekert91-39)[\[102\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-horodecki10-102) but to prove the security of [quantum key distribution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_key_distribution "Quantum key distribution") (QKD) under standard assumptions does not require entanglement;[\[103\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-103) however, the _[device independent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Device-independent_quantum_cryptography "Device-independent quantum cryptography")_ security of QKD is shown exploiting entanglement between the communication partners.[\[104\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-104) In August 2014, Brazilian researcher Gabriela Barreto Lemos, from the University of Vienna, and team were able to "take pictures" of objects using photons that had not interacted with the subjects, but were entangled with photons that did interact with such objects.[\[105\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-105) The idea has been adapted to make infrared images using only standard cameras that are insensitive to infrared.[\[106\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-106)

### Entangled states

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Entangled states")\]

There are several canonical entangled states that appear often in theory and experiments.

For two [qubits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qubits "Qubits"), the [Bell states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_state "Bell state") are

\|Φ±⟩=12(\|0⟩A⊗\|0⟩B±\|1⟩A⊗\|1⟩B){\\displaystyle \|\\Phi ^{\\pm }\\rangle ={\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}(\|0\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|0\\rangle \_{B}\\pm \|1\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|1\\rangle \_{B})}![{\displaystyle |\Phi ^{\pm }\rangle ={\frac {1}{\sqrt {2}}}(|0\rangle _{A}\otimes |0\rangle _{B}\pm |1\rangle _{A}\otimes |1\rangle _{B})}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0c5e3e97f9337b2928160879b1039b554fa4efd2)\|Ψ±⟩=12(\|0⟩A⊗\|1⟩B±\|1⟩A⊗\|0⟩B).{\\displaystyle \|\\Psi ^{\\pm }\\rangle ={\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}(\|0\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|1\\rangle \_{B}\\pm \|1\\rangle \_{A}\\otimes \|0\\rangle \_{B}).}![{\displaystyle |\Psi ^{\pm }\rangle ={\frac {1}{\sqrt {2}}}(|0\rangle _{A}\otimes |1\rangle _{B}\pm |1\rangle _{A}\otimes |0\rangle _{B}).}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c3ad61f595b438e703d3fdf2ff88bbe5946b7e3b)

These four pure states are all maximally entangled and form an [orthonormal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthonormal "Orthonormal") [basis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basis_(linear_algebra) "Basis (linear algebra)") of the Hilbert space of the two qubits.[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rieffel2011-48): 38–39 [\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Nielsen-2010-47): 98  They provide examples of how quantum mechanics can violate [Bell-type inequalities](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell%27s_theorem "Bell's theorem").[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Rieffel2011-48): 62 [\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Nielsen-2010-47): 116

For _M_ \> 2 qubits, the [GHZ state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenberger%E2%80%93Horne%E2%80%93Zeilinger_state "Greenberger–Horne–Zeilinger state") is

\|GHZ⟩=\|0⟩⊗M+\|1⟩⊗M2,{\\displaystyle \|\\mathrm {GHZ} \\rangle ={\\frac {\|0\\rangle ^{\\otimes M}+\|1\\rangle ^{\\otimes M}}{\\sqrt {2}}},}![{\displaystyle |\mathrm {GHZ} \rangle ={\frac {|0\rangle ^{\otimes M}+|1\rangle ^{\otimes M}}{\sqrt {2}}},}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e51e220f2b023d20c1e3d4c7461928e4bbafc1bf)

which reduces to the Bell state \|Φ+⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\Phi ^{+}\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\Phi ^{+}\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4570afa1cbf91ef240b83f9ad1476558042f4361) for _M_ = 2. The traditional GHZ state was defined for _M_ = 3. GHZ states are occasionally extended to [qudits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Qudit "Qudit"), i.e., systems of _d_ rather than 2 dimensions.[\[107\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-107)[\[108\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-108) Also for _M_ \> 2 qubits, there are [spin squeezed states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_squeezing "Spin squeezing"), a class of [squeezed coherent states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Squeezed_coherent_states "Squeezed coherent states") satisfying certain restrictions on the uncertainty of spin measurements, which are necessarily entangled.[\[109\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-109) Spin squeezed states are good candidates for enhancing precision measurements using quantum entanglement.[\[110\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-110)

For two [bosonic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boson "Boson") modes, a [NOON state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NOON_state "NOON state") is

\|ψNOON⟩=\|N⟩a\|0⟩b+\|0⟩a\|N⟩b2.{\\displaystyle \|\\psi \_{\\text{NOON}}\\rangle ={\\frac {\|N\\rangle \_{a}\|0\\rangle \_{b}+\|{0}\\rangle \_{a}\|{N}\\rangle \_{b}}{\\sqrt {2}}}.}![{\displaystyle |\psi _{\text{NOON}}\rangle ={\frac {|N\rangle _{a}|0\rangle _{b}+|{0}\rangle _{a}|{N}\rangle _{b}}{\sqrt {2}}}.}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f8fd5adf9cfd79648f2cceb5f5534b61c91e72b)

This is like the Bell state \|Ψ+⟩{\\displaystyle \|\\Psi ^{+}\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |\Psi ^{+}\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3fb516a603ad0d72c91c8e3e736aaad102d073f2) except the basis states \|0⟩{\\displaystyle \|0\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |0\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ed066a3ad158da0ad6d6a421a606b1c8a35eb95b) and \|1⟩{\\displaystyle \|1\\rangle }![{\displaystyle |1\rangle }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/2f53021ca18e77477ee5bd3c1523e5830189ec5c) have been replaced with "the _N_ photons are in one mode" and "the _N_ photons are in the other mode".[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Kishore2007-111) Finally, there also exist [twin Fock states](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Twin_Fock_states&action=edit&redlink=1 "Twin Fock states (page does not exist)") for bosonic modes, which can be created by feeding a [Fock state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fock_state "Fock state") into two arms leading to a beam splitter. They are the sum of multiple NOON states, and can be used to achieve the [Heisenberg limit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heisenberg_limit "Heisenberg limit").[\[112\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-112) For the appropriately chosen measures of entanglement, Bell, GHZ, and NOON states are maximally entangled while spin squeezed and twin Fock states are only partially entangled.[\[113\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-113)[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Kishore2007-111)[\[114\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-114)

### Methods of creating entanglement

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Methods of creating entanglement")\]

Entanglement is usually created by direct interactions between subatomic particles. These interactions can take numerous forms. One of the most commonly used methods is [spontaneous parametric down-conversion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spontaneous_parametric_down-conversion "Spontaneous parametric down-conversion") to generate a pair of photons entangled in polarization.[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-horodecki2007-1)[\[115\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Shadbolt2012-115) Other methods include the use of a [fiber coupler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fiber-optic_adapter "Fiber-optic adapter") to confine and mix photons, photons emitted from decay cascade of the bi-exciton in a [quantum dot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_dot "Quantum dot"),[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-116) or the use of the [Hong–Ou–Mandel effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hong%E2%80%93Ou%E2%80%93Mandel_effect "Hong–Ou–Mandel effect").[\[117\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-117) Quantum entanglement of a [particle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elementary_particle "Elementary particle") and its [antiparticle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiparticle "Antiparticle"), such as an electron and a [positron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positron "Positron"), can be created by partial overlap of the corresponding [quantum wave functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_wave_function "Quantum wave function") in [Hardy's interferometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hardy%27s_paradox "Hardy's paradox").[\[118\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Hardy1992-118)[\[119\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Georgiev2022-119) In the earliest tests of Bell's theorem, the entangled particles were generated using [atomic cascades](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_cascade "Atomic cascade").[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Clauser-6) It is also possible to create entanglement between quantum systems that never directly interacted, through the use of [entanglement swapping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_teleportation#Entanglement_swapping "Quantum teleportation"). Two independently prepared, identical particles may also be entangled if their wave functions merely spatially overlap, at least partially.[\[120\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-120)

### Testing a system for entanglement

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=22 "Edit section: Testing a system for entanglement")\]

A density matrix _ρ_ is called separable if it can be written as a convex sum of product states, namely
ρ=∑jpjρj(A)⊗ρj(B){\\displaystyle {\\rho =\\sum \_{j}p\_{j}\\rho \_{j}^{(A)}\\otimes \\rho \_{j}^{(B)}}}![{\displaystyle {\rho =\sum _{j}p_{j}\rho _{j}^{(A)}\otimes \rho _{j}^{(B)}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/595cfaf3cbe5191d7b447eecd2003515a410ae2a)
with 0≤pj≤1{\\displaystyle 0\\leq p\_{j}\\leq 1}![{\displaystyle 0\leq p_{j}\leq 1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/be98598a3b3c77ddc979cf9a2617effaa0f62c47) probabilities. By definition, a state is entangled if it is not separable.

For 2-qubit and qubit-qutrit systems (2 × 2 and 2 × 3 respectively) the simple [Peres–Horodecki criterion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peres%E2%80%93Horodecki_criterion "Peres–Horodecki criterion") provides both a necessary and a sufficient criterion for separability, and thus—inadvertently—for detecting entanglement. However, for the general case, the criterion is merely a necessary one for separability, as the problem becomes NP-hard when generalized.[\[121\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-NP-hard1-121)[\[122\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-NP-hard2-122) Other separability criteria include (but not limited to) the [range criterion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Range_criterion "Range criterion"), [reduction criterion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reduction_criterion "Reduction criterion"), and those based on uncertainty relations.[\[123\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-123)[\[124\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-124)[\[125\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-125)[\[126\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-126) See Ref.[\[127\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-127) for a review of separability criteria in discrete-variable systems and Ref.[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-FriisEtAl2019entanglement-128) for a review on techniques and challenges in experimental entanglement certification in discrete-variable systems.

A numerical approach to the problem is suggested by [Jon Magne Leinaas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jon_Magne_Leinaas "Jon Magne Leinaas"), [Jan Myrheim](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jan_Myrheim "Jan Myrheim") and [Eirik Ovrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Eirik_Ovrum&action=edit&redlink=1 "Eirik Ovrum (page does not exist)") in their paper "Geometrical aspects of entanglement".[\[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-129) Leinaas et al. offer a numerical approach, iteratively refining an estimated separable state towards the target state to be tested, and checking if the target state can indeed be reached. In continuous variable systems, the Peres–Horodecki criterion also applies. Specifically, Simon[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-130) formulated a particular version of the Peres–Horodecki criterion in terms of the second-order moments of canonical operators and showed that it is necessary and sufficient for 1⊕1{\\displaystyle 1\\oplus 1}![{\displaystyle 1\oplus 1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/910ec7d56dcdfb30a81400532fa3399299261342)-mode Gaussian states (see Ref.[\[131\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-131) for a seemingly different but essentially equivalent approach). It was later found[\[132\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-132) that Simon's condition is also necessary and sufficient for 1⊕n{\\displaystyle 1\\oplus n}![{\displaystyle 1\oplus n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/49fdecc4a94f6e2bf4ea50e1cebd24d9e346173c)-mode Gaussian states, but no longer sufficient for 2⊕2{\\displaystyle 2\\oplus 2}![{\displaystyle 2\oplus 2}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/60ea3c2d231414b3e14766f90606b747314d3fd8)-mode Gaussian states. Simon's condition can be generalized by taking into account the higher order moments of canonical operators[\[133\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-133)[\[134\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-134) or by using entropic measures.[\[135\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-135)[\[136\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-136)

### In quantum gravity

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: In quantum gravity")\]

There is a fundamental conflict, referred to as the [problem of time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Problem_of_time "Problem of time"), between the way the concept of _time_ is used in quantum mechanics, and the role it plays in [general relativity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_relativity "General relativity"). In standard quantum theories time acts as an independent background through which states evolve, while general relativity treats time as a dynamical variable which relates directly with matter. Part of the effort to reconcile these approaches to time results in the [Wheeler–DeWitt equation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheeler%E2%80%93DeWitt_equation "Wheeler–DeWitt equation"), which predicts the state of the universe is timeless or static, contrary to ordinary experience.[\[137\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Moreva2014-137)
Work started by [Don Page](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Don_Page_(physicist) "Don Page (physicist)") and [William Wootters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Wootters "William Wootters")[\[138\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-138)[\[139\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-139)[\[140\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-140) suggests that the universe appears to evolve for observers on the inside because of energy entanglement between an evolving system and a clock system, both within the universe.[\[137\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Moreva2014-137) In this way the overall system can remain timeless while parts experience time via entanglement. The issue remains an open question closely related to attempts at theories of [quantum gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_gravity "Quantum gravity").[\[141\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-141)[\[142\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-142)

In general relativity, gravity arises from the curvature of spacetime and that curvature derives from the distribution of matter. However, matter is governed by quantum mechanics. Integration of these two theories faces many problems. In an (unrealistic) model space called the [anti-de Sitter space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-de_Sitter_space "Anti-de Sitter space"), the [AdS/CFT correspondence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AdS/CFT_correspondence "AdS/CFT correspondence") allows a quantum gravitational system to be related to a quantum field theory without gravity.[\[143\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-Swingle2018-143) Using this correspondence, [Mark Van Raamsdonk](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_Van_Raamsdonk "Mark Van Raamsdonk") suggested that [spacetime](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacetime "Spacetime") arises as an emergent phenomenon of the quantum degrees of freedom that are entangled and live in the boundary of the spacetime.[\[144\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-144)

## Experiments demonstrating and using entanglement

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Experiments demonstrating and using entanglement")\]

### Bell tests

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Bell tests")\]

Main article: [Bell test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_test "Bell test")

A [Bell test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell_test "Bell test"), also known as _Bell inequality test_ or _Bell experiment_, is a real-world physics experiment designed to test the theory of quantum mechanics against the hypothesis of local hidden variables. These tests empirically evaluate the implications of [Bell's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bell%27s_theorem "Bell's theorem"). To date, all Bell tests have found that the hypothesis of local hidden variables is inconsistent with the way that physical systems behave. Many types of Bell tests have been performed in physics laboratories, often with the goal of ameliorating problems of experimental design or set-up that could in principle affect the validity of the findings of earlier Bell tests. This is known as "closing loopholes in Bell tests". In earlier tests, it could not be ruled out that the result at one point could have been subtly transmitted to the remote point, affecting the outcome at the second location;[\[9\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:2-9) however, "loophole-free" Bell tests have since been performed where the locations were sufficiently separated that communications at the speed of light would have taken longer—in one case, 10,000 times longer—than the interval between the measurements.[\[8\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:1-8)[\[7\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-:0-7)[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-NTR-20151021-15)[\[36\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-hanson-36)

In 2017, Yin et al. reported setting a new quantum entanglement distance record of 1,203 km, demonstrating the survival of a two-photon pair and a violation of a Bell inequality, reaching a [CHSH valuation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CHSH_inequality "CHSH inequality") of 2.37±0.09, under strict Einstein locality conditions, from the [Micius satellite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_Experiments_at_Space_Scale "Quantum Experiments at Space Scale") to bases in Lijian, Yunnan and Delingha, Qinghai, increasing the efficiency of transmission over prior fiberoptic experiments by an order of magnitude.[\[145\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-145)[\[146\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-146)

### Entanglement of top quarks

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Entanglement of top quarks")\]

In 2023 the [LHC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Large_Hadron_Collider "Large Hadron Collider") using techniques from [quantum tomography](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_tomography "Quantum tomography") measured entanglement at the highest energy so far,[\[147\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-147)[\[148\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-148)[\[149\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-149) a rare intersection between quantum information and high energy physics based on theoretical work first proposed in 2021.[\[150\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-150) The experiment was carried by the [ATLAS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ATLAS_experiment "ATLAS experiment") detector measuring the spin of top-quark pair production and the effect was observed with a more than 5 [_σ_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Standard_deviation "Standard deviation") level of significance, the top quark is the heaviest known particle and therefore has a very short lifetime (τ{\\displaystyle \\tau }![{\displaystyle \tau }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/38a7dcde9730ef0853809fefc18d88771f95206c) ≈ 10−25 s) being the only quark that decays before undergoing [hadronization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadronization "Hadronization") (~ 10−23 s) and spin decorrelation (~ 10−21 s), so the spin information is transferred without much loss to the leptonic decays products that will be caught by the detector.[\[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-151) The [spin polarization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spin_polarization "Spin polarization") and correlation of the particles was measured and tested for entanglement with [concurrence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concurrence_(quantum_computing) "Concurrence (quantum computing)") as well as the [Peres–Horodecki criterion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peres%E2%80%93Horodecki_criterion "Peres–Horodecki criterion") and subsequently the effect has been confirmed too in the [CMS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CMS_experiment "CMS experiment") detector.[\[152\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-152)[\[153\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-153)

### Entanglement of macroscopic objects

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Entanglement of macroscopic objects")\]

In 2020, researchers reported the quantum entanglement between the [motion of a millimetre-sized mechanical oscillator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vibrations_of_a_circular_membrane "Vibrations of a circular membrane") and a disparate distant spin system of a cloud of atoms.[\[154\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-154) Later work complemented this work by quantum-entangling two mechanical oscillators.[\[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-155)[\[156\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-156)[\[157\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-157)

### Entanglement of quarks and gluons in protons

\[ [edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Quantum_entanglement&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Entanglement of quarks and gluons in protons")\]

Physicists at [Brookhaven National Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brookhaven_National_Laboratory "Brookhaven National Laboratory") demonstrated quantum entanglement within [protons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Protons "Protons"), showing [quarks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quarks "Quarks") and [gluons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gluons "Gluons") are interdependent rather than isolated particles.[\[158\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-158) Using high-energy electron-proton collisions, they revealed maximal entanglement, reshaping our understanding of proton structure.[\[159\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_entanglement#cite_note-159)

To avoid such action at a distance they have to attribute, to the space-time regions in question, real properties in advance of observation, correlated properties, which predetermine the outcomes of these particular observations. Since these real properties, fixed in advance of observation, are not contained in quantum formalism, that formalism for EPR is incomplete. It may be correct, as far as it goes, but the usual quantum formalism cannot be the whole story."
